<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1d4c7264-3535-4bfb-9691-93529a426231'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1452553315" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1452553315" />
    <a title="Komal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17030658-komal"><img alt="Komal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1448554122p2/17030658.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1452553315?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Komal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Komal" href="/user/show/17030658-komal">Komal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover  9789960850894" href="/book/show/544852.You_Can_Be_The_Happiest_Woman_in_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1452553315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2230473644449819276"><b> HERE WE GO AGAIN. </b> <br/><br/>For every good religious person I meet, there are 50 morons, and seemingly half of the latter have published a book. I’d like to draw attention to one of these books- once again about telling Muslim women how to live their lives. (Instructions include “SMILE! PRAY! BE GRATEFUL!” Yeah ok. So helpful.)<br/><br/>Optimistically titled <b> “You Can Be The Happiest Woman In the World” </b> , the book presents an interesting look into the starkly primeval mind of a strange little man and his opinion</span>
  <span id="freeText2230473644449819276" style="display:none"><b> HERE WE GO AGAIN. </b> <br /><br />For every good religious person I meet, there are 50 morons, and seemingly half of the latter have published a book. I’d like to draw attention to one of these books- once again about telling Muslim women how to live their lives. (Instructions include “SMILE! PRAY! BE GRATEFUL!” Yeah ok. So helpful.)<br /><br />Optimistically titled <b> “You Can Be The Happiest Woman In the World” </b> , the book presents an interesting look into the starkly primeval mind of a strange little man and his opinions on women. It was first published in 2004, which is weird. I expected it to be far older, judging by the content. I mean the only occupation it suggests is cattle rearing. Who is the target audience? Poor farm women? The book also refers to itself as <i> “A Treasure Chest of Reminders”. </i> <br /><br />It doesn’t mention what these reminders are of. Presumably God, but for me it conjured up reminders of Medieval pastoral scenes full of disease and war.<br /><br /><b> <u> The Review: </u></b><br /><br />The first thing that gave me a sense of foreboding was the cover. My copy has a murky green background and a dark, shapeless form that on closer inspection is the silhouette of a veiled woman clutching a Quran. It looks unintentionally terrifying, like the cover of a bad horror novel.<br /><br /><b> Introduction </b><br /><br />Still, I approached this book with an open mind. Sometimes religious instruction books are kind of uplifting, or at least focus on the importance of the spiritual over the material. The introduction, for example, started off well, promising to bring hope to women in despair, address her <i> “rational mind and pure heart” </i> and not to care about <i> “worldly adornments, false appearances or transient fashion.” </i><br />But then it abruptly changes tack and launches into a harangue about evil western literature. O_O Wait what happened? <br /><br /><i> <b> “The way to happiness is to be found in clarity of knowledge and soundness of education. This cannot be achieved by romantic fiction which leads to frustration and depression. Indeed, the matter is more serious than that, such as the stories of Agatha Christie, which teach about deceit, crime and robbery. I have read the series entitled The Best of World Fiction, a selection of exciting, Nobel-prize winning stories, and I found that these stories contained many serious mistakes and a great deal of foolishness. How many people have fallen victim to articles or stories they have read.” </b></i> <br /><br />Yes well okay. In the introduction alone we can see this man’s disdain for those silly foolish Nobel laureates, and his presumable conviction that the entire lot of innocent women who read Agatha Christie are now out there, probably committing murder.<br /><br /><b> Chapter 1 </b><br /><br />After the introduction, chapter ones begins. First up is a single page that excitedly proclaims “WELCOME!” in all caps, under which are ten other welcomes <i> (“Welcome, O’ mother of heroes and producer of men.”) </i> After that is a single page that announces the word “YES!” in equally jubilant all-caps. Followed by ten more sentences starting with yes (<i> “YES to your beautiful smile that sends a message of warmth and friendliness to others. </i>) And then a page that exclaims “NO!” You get the picture. <br /><br />After the strange YES NO lists is a 2-pager titled Ten Thoughts to Ponder About. One of my favorites from these ten thoughts is the inexplicable bullet point 6, which says <i> <b> “Be like a tall tree with high aims; if a stone is thrown at it, it simply lets its fruits drop.” </b></i>  I admit, I pondered this for a long time, because it sounded so insane. Out of all the motivational speeches and instagram quotes I’ve ever read, I was never once told to be a stone-pelted, fruit-dropping high-aiming goddamned tree.<br /><br />Then is a single page titled “THINK ABOUT IT…”, with ten more bullet points to think about. I admit I pondered here as well, mostly about why these were under a separate heading instead of under the Ponder About section. It begins by instructing us to <i> <b> “be like the bee, which lands on fragrant flowers and fresh branches”. </b></i>  What does this MEAN? Be near clean good-smelling people? Avoid old people? What is with all these ridiculous similes?<br /><br /><b> Chapter 2 </b><br />Right after “THINK ABOUT IT…” begins chapter 2. At this point I realize there is absolutely no point in organizing this book into chapters, because there is no reading order, and no sense of categorization. Every chapter is the same, with a few short anecdotes under an absolutely irrelevant heading, the paper-back equivalent of clickbait titles. <br /><br />Chapter 2 for example, has a section titled “You have a huge wealth of blessings” but then doesn’t actually say what they are- instead it rants poetically about how <i> <b> “the clouds of worry will be blown away, the darkness of distress will be dispelled, and calamities will come to an end.” </b></i>  and ends by rhetorically demanding how often a woman gets talked about because of her good attitude, obedience to husband, and her modesty when she moves into a new neighborhood. <br /><br />Another, provocative title is “The believing woman and the disbelieving woman are not equal”. Uh oh, here comes the arrogance. Sure enough, the writer says believing women from the believing lands are totes amaze while disbelieving women from the disbelieving lands are <i> <b> “cheap worthless products at offer in all places.” </b></i>  I, incredulous woman from the Incredulous lands, read it with a mixture of amusement and shock. <i> <b> “she is ignorant and foolish, a mere fashion model. She has no value, honor or religion.” </b></i>  Ok, wow. Let’s hope you can see what’s wrong with that statement and move on. <br />The rant ends with a very mysterious line that says <i> <b> “All people will live, the one who lives in a palace and the one who lives in a hovel. But who is the one who is happy?” </b></i>  You guys. This is rhetorical. Clearly the answer is the therapeutic, depression-curing hovel. For outright euphoria try a shipwrecked raft in the middle of the ocean it is BLISS.<br /><br />In fairness, some of the writings are so enthusiastic that they are a bit uplifting. One section talks about how lucky you are to have been born Muslim, rather than any other religion. It says <i> <b> “Congratulations, for you offer five daily prayers, you fast, you go on pilgrimage to the house, you observe hijab. Congratulations, for you are pleased with Allah as your lord, Islam as your religion, and Muhammad SAW as your prophet.” </b></i>  This exuberant joy is very endearing, and if there are people out there who can feel this happiness in being Muslim I am glad of it. May everyone feel happy in what they believe.<br /><br />But this sort of feel-good stuff is rather sparse. Most of the book is sermonic and wildly scatter-brained. A section called “Laziness is kin to failure” starts off by saying hey ladies work hard, and take care of your appearance. But right in the middle of this lecture the author has an outburst of sexism and cries <i> <b> “beware of sin! For it leads to grief, especially the sins that are very common among women.” </b></i>  What could these female-centric sins possibly be you ask? <i> <b> “Forbidden glances, wanton adornment, cursing, slandering, backbiting, denying one’s husbands rights” </b></i>  etc etc. Because of course. This is what women do. Sit around, seduce men and talk shit about people. Men never do this because they are out there committing male-related crimes such as killing and stealing I suppose.<br /><br /><b> Chapter 3 </b><br />One of my favorite anecdotes because of its sheer insanity is titled “Old Women make heroes.” Such an eye-catching title. It brings to mind legendary warrior women or the likes of mother Teresa. Except you can kill your expectations right there, because this author’s prattling meandering idiocy doesn’t expose anything remotely heroic. These are quite literally a bunch of stories about women who are old. Yes, their sole collective defining trait is that they are old. They don’t even have names.<br /><br />The first heroic old woman was told that al-Hajjaj would kill her son. To that, pat came her reply <i> <b> “If you do not kill him, he will die anyway.” </b></i>  WOW lady, way to be a sociopath. This does not sound like heroism to me, it sounds like terrible parenting. Maybe the author mistranslated the message or something because my first unadulterated impression is of a woman who doesn’t care if her son dies. Because he will die someday anyway. Happily, the author highlights what the important heroic bit was- <b> “putting her trust in her Lord.” </b> By telling al-Hajjaj that he could murder her son. WHERE does the trust in god part come in here???<br /><br />The second anecdote is even more ridiculous. The author raves that we should <i> <b> “be like the old Persian woman who put her trust in Allah the day she had to be away from her chicken coop. She looked up towards the heavens and said ‘O Allah, protect my chicken coop for you are the best of protectors!” </b></i>  That’s it. That’s the entire story of Heroic Old Woman #2. Now I understand that the message here is to trust that god will protect your material possessions, and that is fine, but what is even remotely heroic about leaving a chicken coop unguarded? In an age of feminism, gender equality, and thousands of towering female figures, is THIS what the author is giving me to look upon as heroism?? Is this man unaware of what a hero is? Are there no better female role models in Islam that he could have turned to, rather than nameless old women who are coldly dismissive of their actual children but overprotective of chickens, and who I suspect he made up himself? <br /><br /><b> Final thoughts: <b> <br /><br />I just have to add that the portrayal of women in this book is troubling. Every woman is described in three ways- a mother, wife and a devout woman. She doesn’t really do anything other than worship and obey her husband. She has no job, no ambition, no character, and all her problems can be solved by being grateful. What about women who are actually depressed or troubled, and sincerely picked this book up to lift their spirits? It won’t do anything for them. It’s designed for very simple minded, impressionably young and married women.<br /><br />It also mentions fairly often that a woman should rush to make her husband feel better. If he comes home stressed or tired she must wait on him hand and foot, and then try to solve his problem. Many women seem to have a problem with this notion, although I don’t understand why. If you remove the wife/husband and replace it with human/human it says one human sees that the other human is stressed, and tries to make that human feel better. This is GOOD. This should be encouraged. I don’t understand why PLEASING the husband is being confused with servility these days. Unfortunately, I think he said something nice by mistake and not by intention. Because the rest of the book is just super weird about the concept of being a wife- basically the guy you marry owns you.<br /><br />There’s one story in particular that comes to mind- a woman goes crying to her dad and says sometimes she doesn’t listen to her husband and so she is scared to go to hell. Her father tells her yes, if she dies while her husband is displeased with her, she is being chucked straight into hell. Then the author tacks on some message about how the key to paradise is obeying the hubby. Yes. To review, a sobbing woman is terrified of going to hell because she doesn’t listen to her husband, and her father tells her lol yeah you’re totes going to hell for it. Very comforting. I’m sure heartbroken sobbing women love being told to obey a guy by another guy under the threat of eternal torment.<br /><br />For some reason, this book also romanticizes poverty. It insists over and over that “poor women in mud huts” are happier than people who “live in mansions and sleep in silk sheets” because the poor woman believes in god and implies that somehow owning material wealth equals being astray from God. I wonder if he has ever met with or spoken to these happy poor women. Because I have, and the stories they tell chill me to my bones. The horrors they endure, the squalor they live in, the misery that they cope with- how can this author just gloss over it all and insist that they are happy? Certainly they are religious. Who needs religion more than the poor? How will they forge through life without a beautiful belief to cling to? Instead of reducing their struggle to a silly proverb, he could have spoken about empathizing with them or helping them, which Islam does stress on so deeply.<br /><br />To be fair, you can tell the intentions behind the book are good, and it is trying to focus on things like the importance of inner beauty and trust in God, but its attempts are marred by the author’s poor writing ability and narrow range of thought. It is circumscribed within the same hackneyed anecdotes about camel-riding burqa-clad cattle-rearing uninspiring women from oral tradition. It has no wisdom, kindness, truth or beauty within it. It reads rather like a set of demanded instructions, without any empathy at all. The language used is uncannily like that of badly translated hadis, which is perplexingly common in modern Islamic books (why don’t people re-translate hadis into better, simpler English?). It is as if these authors rely solely on old Arabic translations to wield the English language.<br /><br /><br /><b> Verdict </b><br /><br />avoid this junk. Your brain cells will commit hara kiri</b></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="2230473644449819276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/376947655?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaliya" href="/user/show/7279490-aaliya">Aaliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7279490-aaliya?shelf=religion">religion</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer376947655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15648990692185034931">This book suffers from a disjointed and chaotic structure. The author can be condescending and simplistic in his core messages, one of which encourages the reader to think of those who are less happy (and surely not be glad about it, for that would be Schadenfreude) and to be grateful that their own life isn't worse. There are many old-fashioned views with regards to the role of women; stay at home, obey your husband, avoid typical female vices like gossiping. The book takes a condemnatory tone </span>
  <span id="freeText15648990692185034931" style="display:none">This book suffers from a disjointed and chaotic structure. The author can be condescending and simplistic in his core messages, one of which encourages the reader to think of those who are less happy (and surely not be glad about it, for that would be Schadenfreude) and to be grateful that their own life isn't worse. There are many old-fashioned views with regards to the role of women; stay at home, obey your husband, avoid typical female vices like gossiping. The book takes a condemnatory tone when discussing Western women's clothes and their frivolous pursuits - basically anything that doesn't involve praying or serving your husband and family.<br />The quotes from the Qur'an and Hadith are mostly relevant and interesting. Some of the stories are particularly inspiring, such as those of women soldiers. But few of them have been interpreted with any depth or with any regard to context.<br />In short, this book had a lot of potential. But it isn't a great read for women, and it didn't make me happy.</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText15174058658555770943" style="display:none">If you have ever had depressing thoughts, low motivation, or if you feel stress on a regular basis, you are not alone. Dr. al-Qarni provides inspiration for all situations in life that can cause us grief. Tragedies often make us realize that in order to be the best wife, mother and woman possible, we must take care of ourselves and those that we love, as if our lives depend on it. We must remember the lessons that some of the greatest women in Islamic history have taught us by example. We must rely on Allah to give us strength when we can’t find it in our own lives. Within this book lies the encouragement to be the leading woman in all areas of your life. You will find peace and happiness by holding on to the rope of Allah. The author reminds us that when we take one step closer to Allah, He will take ten more to be closer to you.<br /><br />This book is also available in Filipino, French, German, Indonesian, Malay, Spanish, and Turkish translations by the publisher, International Islamic Publishing House at <a href="http://www.iiph.com.sa" rel="nofollow noopener">www.iiph.com</a>. </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19959790-farah-ali?shelf=islamic-books">islamic-books</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1207090743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10384876860407691194">Part of the blurb of this book reads "a treasure chest of reminders that will help you learn important lessons". This book is filled with short reminders both philosophical and Islamic. I found the Islamic ones which referenced the Qur'an, Sunnah and pious predecessors to be the most impactful. I found it harder to get through the other ones. The organisation of this book seemed quite random to me. It has pages listed chapter 1, 2, 3 etc (chapter headings would have been better), each chapter ha</span>
  <span id="freeText10384876860407691194" style="display:none">Part of the blurb of this book reads "a treasure chest of reminders that will help you learn important lessons". This book is filled with short reminders both philosophical and Islamic. I found the Islamic ones which referenced the Qur'an, Sunnah and pious predecessors to be the most impactful. I found it harder to get through the other ones. The organisation of this book seemed quite random to me. It has pages listed chapter 1, 2, 3 etc (chapter headings would have been better), each chapter has 10 subsections within it, each of them have a heading. The chapters are without any theme, it reads one random reminder (for each subsection) after another throughout the book. These subsections have a quote to open with and a quote to end with. I noticed that most of these quotes are not linked in with the reminder of that subsection. I find this book would have been better had there been a general theme for each chapter with concepts linked to one another and similarly quotes being linked to the reminder being discussed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10384876860407691194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer357865126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15241606421271122477">This is a religious based Non-fiction book for Muslim girls.<br/>My dad brought me and my mom this book as a gift. Words CANNOT describe how wonderful and great AND valuable this book is to me. Every girl should read this book, even if your not Muslim because it will never fail to make you feel great about yourself and the people around you. It's not the kind of non-fiction you say "oH! I already read it," but it's like a study guide to keep you smiling. The kind of book you go back to all the time! </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2399177680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2399177680" />
    <a title="Delilah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9995007-delilah"><img alt="Delilah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599754718p2/9995007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2399177680?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Delilah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Delilah" href="/user/show/9995007-delilah">Delilah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover  9789960850894" href="/book/show/544852.You_Can_Be_The_Happiest_Woman_in_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9995007-delilah?shelf=feed-the-soul">feed-the-soul</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9995007-delilah?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2399177680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2213590062181637722">Well, that was uncomfortable. I'm sure the author had great intentions, and bless him for that, but this book is just chaotic from beginning to end.<br/> <br/>The whole book is so disorganized and scatter-brained. There are random quotes at the end of each chapter/section that have nothing to do with the topics discussed in the paragraphs preceding them. I'm not sure what their purpose is or who even said these things in the first place. The worst part is that half of them don't even make sense. The autho</span>
  <span id="freeText2213590062181637722" style="display:none">Well, that was uncomfortable. I'm sure the author had great intentions, and bless him for that, but this book is just chaotic from beginning to end.<br /> <br />The whole book is so disorganized and scatter-brained. There are random quotes at the end of each chapter/section that have nothing to do with the topics discussed in the paragraphs preceding them. I'm not sure what their purpose is or who even said these things in the first place. The worst part is that half of them don't even make sense. The author's view of women is simplistic and often condescending, which makes me an unhappy rather than happy woman. He says some absolutely astounding and disrespectful things about disbelieving women in sweeping generalizations about their habits and customs. He discourages basically everything except being a dutiful homebound wife and mother.<br /><br />There are some stories and anecdotes thrown in for "inspiration" but many of them seem unrealistic and fabricated just for funsies. He also romanticizes poverty by comparing the poor and the rich and insisting that the poor man is happier in every case. Other times, he encourages moderation in wealth. It's difficult to follow his train of thought. His oversimplified writing style, stories, and absurd similes are somewhat insulting and imply that he views women as feeble-minded. There are a few uplifting passages with references to the Qur'an, Sunnah, and the pious predecessors, but these are ultimately spoiled by the overall sense of disorganization.<br /><br />Two stars for the good intention, and for the reminders that I felt were useful. There were a few things I read here that I will try to keep in mind. I may actually pull this off my shelf on a particularly stressful day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2213590062181637722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2189637014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2189637014" />
    <a title="Deenplusbook" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74012389-deenplusbook"><img alt="Deenplusbook" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513765901p2/74012389.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2189637014?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deenplusbook" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deenplusbook" href="/user/show/74012389-deenplusbook">Deenplusbook</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover  9789960850894" href="/book/show/544852.You_Can_Be_The_Happiest_Woman_in_the_World">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Specially to sisters, wives, mothers, but to every muslim in general</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74012389-deenplusbook?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2189637014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9493711265650895301">Everyone of us are striving everyday to become a "better" person, and sometimes its as easy as flipping a coin while other days you dont even want to lift your blanket. And all you need is someone who can cheer you up, and encourage you to stand up and fight with you against whatever you are going through. At these moments, our minds get so filled up with our thoughts, we sometimes forget that, Allah is always there for us and we are never alone in our struggles. This book remind us of Allah and</span>
  <span id="freeText9493711265650895301" style="display:none">Everyone of us are striving everyday to become a "better" person, and sometimes its as easy as flipping a coin while other days you dont even want to lift your blanket. And all you need is someone who can cheer you up, and encourage you to stand up and fight with you against whatever you are going through. At these moments, our minds get so filled up with our thoughts, we sometimes forget that, Allah is always there for us and we are never alone in our struggles. This book remind us of Allah and our purpose of life, through short and meaningful reminders.<br />The book contains a total of 14 chapters with short sections along with eye catching short titles. Each section is focused on a specific area, highlighting real events from islamic history as well as western people, verses of Quran, Hadiths, and also inspiring quotes at the end of each section. The book as a whole explains, reasons that might have led you to or lead you to your sadness, anxiety, depression or anything that is blocking your way to true happiness, along with ways to overcome those. Moreover, through the real life events and guidance to reflect upon our own blessings, it creates a path for us to remember Allah at all times of our lives.<br />I would personally recommend this book. The structure of the book is really helpful, mostly at times you wouldn't prefer to do a lot of reading to understand the message of a book. You also do not need to finish the book in one go, take your time and refer to it at times you feel and want to lighten up your spirituality (eeman) and to be more motivated to do more.<br /><br />Jazak'Allah Khair<br />You can follow me on twitter and instagram, @deenplusbook for more islamic book reviews</span>

<div id="review_2704161651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2704161651" />
    <a title="Ms. Sleek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89164826-ms-sleek"><img alt="Ms. Sleek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552497675p2/89164826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2704161651?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Sleek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Sleek" href="/user/show/89164826-ms-sleek">Ms. Sleek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9789960850900" href="/book/show/1111639.You_Can_Be_The_Happiest_Woman_In_The_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2704161651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8943600624762241546">A <i>lovely</i> book, not written for everybody...<br/><br/>Throughout reading this book, there were many passages that resonated with me. I kept a small notebook next to me while reading to write down the quotes and messages delivered through the text of this book. I quite enjoyed it, although there are a few points I would suggest to improve the delivery of the idea.<br/><br/>I don't recommend this book be read by "beginner" Muslims, newly converted Muslims, or anyone that is not <i>at least</i> moderately familiar about Islam</span>
  <span id="freeText8943600624762241546" style="display:none">A <i>lovely</i> book, not written for everybody...<br /><br />Throughout reading this book, there were many passages that resonated with me. I kept a small notebook next to me while reading to write down the quotes and messages delivered through the text of this book. I quite enjoyed it, although there are a few points I would suggest to improve the delivery of the idea.<br /><br />I don't recommend this book be read by "beginner" Muslims, newly converted Muslims, or anyone that is not <i>at least</i> moderately familiar about Islam. This book presents Islamic ideologies about family structures, women's positions and roles in society and at home, as well as other concepts involving both men and women and how they can improve their quality of life. <br /><br />I would say that <u>this book is more for an advanced audience</u>. It may seem a little bit condescending to those who don't have a solid foundation about Islam and read this as their first exposure, especially towards women. The book suggests things like women staying at home, etc., which might be offensive or seen as "backwards" to Western women. Personally, I agreed with most of these points, however I do think that the delivery could've been smoother.<br /><br />I will give the benefit of the doubt to the fact that this book was originally written in Arabic, and perhaps the translation to English shifted the tone to a less sensitive feel. However, considering the topics discussed, there probably isn't a sensitive approach to delivering the hard facts. <br /><br />Although this book is technically written for women, I think men could <b>DEFINITELY</b> benefit from the text as well since the majority of the concepts involve life perspectives and motivational stories, quotes, and passages from the Qur'an. Many of the stories of the <i>sahaba</i> and others were quite new to me, so it was refreshing reading about inspiring life events/moments of others that hold high significance and examples of how to approach different circumstances in one's life. <br /><br />The layout of the text was quite random to be honest. The book is divided into chapters, but these chapters didn't seem to have a certain theme? Maybe I failed to connect the dots, but one paragraph or one page would talk about one thing, and the next page discusses something entirely different. Maybe a little organization would help improve the delivery. <br /><br />Overall, I did enjoy reading this book. As a practicing Muslim woman, there were a few eye-openers for me while reading and I would recommend it to my circle of friends. If you do read this book, don't take every suggestion to heart or personally. Try to understand the point of the Author and realize that the message is intended for <i>your</i> benefit. <br /><br /><i>The cover of this book that I own has white roses on it. The book is translated in English.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8943600624762241546" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2899291424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2899291424" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19926958-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537479219p2/19926958.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2899291424?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/19926958-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2899291424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7746747050142270987">This book is a light, feel-good read. I wasn't expecting much going into it, but I had been getting overwhelmed by a lot of small things that had been adding up and this book did a good job of helping me see the positives over the negative. <br/><br/>But I do have one major complaint - women are almost only spoken of as wives or mothers and it speaks against non-Muslims and those that don't wear hijab. This was definitely frustrating, but it wasn't so much that I couldn't just skip past those sections. <br/><br/>I </span>
  <span id="freeText7746747050142270987" style="display:none">This book is a light, feel-good read. I wasn't expecting much going into it, but I had been getting overwhelmed by a lot of small things that had been adding up and this book did a good job of helping me see the positives over the negative. <br /><br />But I do have one major complaint - women are almost only spoken of as wives or mothers and it speaks against non-Muslims and those that don't wear hijab. This was definitely frustrating, but it wasn't so much that I couldn't just skip past those sections. <br /><br />I gave it 4/5 because overall it was what I was looking for, but it does have some serious flaws in the way it discusses women.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7746747050142270987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XyxNXBjzqs1QHurC+D/KqTbwlnm25K7gqU5V+l3mY2SB2Y0TcGxZZTOvrAia5jnBFfKRajL0Twc2mAEzQl5jCQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iQjCwfLtiDj+e7KkKLAmqpQqbpmc72oEiwvQRZBjfEZX/QKOmnJ7kJ3K9G5KadXCtyhpihj/i+MU3YSMj9t8Kw==')})
  }

&quot;تؤذينني عمداً وكأنك تفرغ في نفسي أحقادك وأوجاعك وأمراضك، تؤذيني عمداً باسم الحبّ..&quot;. وهي بالرغم من كل الحقائق والوقائع تعترف: &quot;لم أبكِ بحرقة إلا بسببك ولم أضحك من أعماقي إلا معك..       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1201952._">About عائض القرني</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1201952._"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1456419436p3/1201952.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1201952._">عائض القرني
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,925
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3787178923269316233">عائض بن عبد الله القرني داعية إسلامي من السعودية، وصاحب كتاب لا تحزن الذي حقق نسبة مبيعات عالية، وكان إمام وخطيب جامع أبي بكر الصديق بأبها. وهو صاحب منهج وسطي لأهل السنة والجماعة من مواليد قرية آل شريح من آل سليمان في سبت العلايا عام 1379هـ/1960م. درس الابتدائية في مدرسة آل سليمان، ثم درس المتوسطة في المعهد العلمي بالرياض، ودرس الثانوية في المعهد العلمي بأبها، وتخرج من كلية أصول الدين بأبها، وحضر </span>
  <span id="freeText3787178923269316233" style="display:none">عائض بن عبد الله القرني داعية إسلامي من السعودية، وصاحب كتاب لا تحزن الذي حقق نسبة مبيعات عالية، وكان إمام وخطيب جامع أبي بكر الصديق بأبها. وهو صاحب منهج وسطي لأهل السنة والجماعة من مواليد قرية آل شريح من آل سليمان في سبت العلايا عام 1379هـ/1960م. درس الابتدائية في مدرسة آل سليمان، ثم درس المتوسطة في المعهد العلمي بالرياض، ودرس الثانوية في المعهد العلمي بأبها، وتخرج من كلية أصول الدين بأبها، وحضر لشهادة الماجستير في رسالة بعنوان: كتاب البدعة وأثرها في الدراية والرواية، ثم حضر لشهادة الدكتوراه في تحقيق المُفهِم على مختصر صحيح مسلم. له أكثر من 800 خطبة صوتية إسلامية في الدروس والمحاضرات والأمسيات الشعرية والندوات الأدبية.<br />-------------------------<br />A'id al-Qarni is animam and preacher of the Mosque of Abu Bakr Abha.<br /><br />He went to the primary school in Al-Suleiman, continued his education on the Scientific Institute in Riyadh, on the high school in the Scientific Institute in Abha, and earned a master's degree from the Faculty of Theology in Abha.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3787178923269316233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
